B001N1DPDE|5.0|This a a hefty knife that feels great in the hands. You really feel confident in it once you hold it. With a 1/4inch and 1 pound weight, it sure excels in batoning small diameter logs,prying, and light chopping. The big belly makes it a wonderful kitchen knife when it is not hacking pieces of wood. I've used it to open young coconuts which are surprisingly hard and this knife has no problem piercing the shell and prying the top off. The extended pommel allows me to use a rubber mallet to drive it into the coconuts. Tip strength is great. Could be sharper out of the box but nothing a little touching up can't take care of. If you want a good all-around knife and don't mind carrying the 1 pound heft (which in my opinions gives more advantages than disadvantages) this is your knife. Lastly, at such a low price point, it is a must have. The sheath is ok but I found that it retained the knife a little too tightly, making it difficult to get out. I used a heat gun to make the pressure retention point a little softer and then cooled it wedged a little wider so it wouldn't hold the knife too tightly, but still tight enough to prevent the knife from slipping out when carried upside down. Also I have read that the sheath has a design flaw in that it dulls the knife every time you take the knife out and put it back in since the edge of the knife cuts into the sheath. I haven't seen this yet with my sheath so it might be a quality control issue but still experimenting. Many others have gotten a customized kydex sheath to take care of this problem.

0062183591|5.0|This is Ariely's third book. He does not rest on his laurels though and this book is just as good as his previous ones. Ariely challenges what appears obvious to earthlings. He designs creative studies to test whether our most obvious gut feelings are indeed true. What readers find may be both surprising and incredibly entertaining. Ariely's lessons can be used to improve different aspects of your organization, society or personal life.<br /><br />I found the section on the effect of wearing fake brand names on honesty particularly amusing. Earlier on Ariely pointed out how pharmaceutical reps manipulate physicians by various strategies (ie: free lunches for them and their staff, golf trips, etc...). Then he talked about how he gave a talk for the fashion industry and he was subsequently given a free Prada handbag as a thank you gift. Later on he was provided with samples of designer handbags and sunglasses (he borrowed the items) as part of a study that he designed. Could these services have affected the way Ariely designed his study and his conclusions? As he demonstrated convincingly in his book humans can be manipulated in various ways. In fact you would expect a behavioral economist to point out the ridiculousness of spending money on expensive brand name items (for example, he has pointed out the irrationality of spending money on expensive wines in the past). But his study puts a positive light on wearing genuine brand names. I am not doubting that there are positive aspects of wearing genuine brands and negative aspects of wearing fakes. I just wonder whether what a researcher chooses to emphasize may be affected by favors. He concludes that people who wear fake brand names are more dishonest. Could he have designed a different study to demonstrate that in general people who wear high price brands are dishonest? My guess is that given the kindness of the folks who provided him with the brands, he would be hesitant to reach such a conclusion. For purity sake,I would have preferred if he had actually bought the samples for his study instead of getting them for free. That certainly would be very expensive. His getting free samples for his study is no different than when researchers are paid by the pharmaceutical companies to study their drugs. By the way, I am a physician and admit that I have been manipulated by pharmaceutical reps in the past.<br /><br />Ariely is very enthusiastic about his work and it is contagious. In this book he tackles honesty. I did not agree with all his interpretations of his studies and presume many other readers will not always agree either. But we have to remember that he is tackling a social science where there is room  for different interpretations. Nevertheless, he makes you think and question your gut feelings. This was a very delightful book and probably among the top three books I have read in the past 12 months. Strongly recommended.

1455502758|5.0|It came as no surprise that one of the greatest and most remarkable troublemakers and polemicists Britain has ever produced didn't leave without having a few important things to say. The late great Christopher Hitchens used the pages of Vanity Fair during his battle against a tumor in his esophagus to partly apply the maxim of Dylan Thomas to \\"rage, rage against the dying of the light\\". That said you sense throughout the pages of \\"Mortality\\", a book collecting those special essays, that Hitchens instinctively felt that this was one argument he wasn't going to win. As such his tangle with death is a level headed but poignant dalliance with the slow degradation of a body which graphically charts the \\"wager\\" with chemotherapy taking \\"your taste buds, your ability to concentrate, your ability to digest and the hair on your head\\". He is painfully honest and reflective throughout about his predicament not least the \\"gnawing sense of waste\\" and the reality of becoming an early \\"finalist in the race of life\\". Yet it wouldn't be Hitchens if the opportunity for settling some old scores was not taken and in particular his restatement of his vociferous views on atheism despite the fact that September 20th 2010 was designated by one religious website as \\"Everyone pray for Hitchens day\\".<br /><br />Others were less charitable for in some quarters at the onset of Hitchens illness produced a vicious form of schadenfreude not least amongst his many enemies in the Christian right where his strong opinions on religion had provoked and outraged those not prepared to countenance any debate. He quotes an opinion from an religious blog that viewed his throat cancer as \\"Gods revenge for him using his voice to blaspheme him\\". Undoubtedly most Christians would find such a view repugnant but in any case Hitchens would have no truck with such nonsense. In his autobiography \\"Hitch 22\\" he was candid about a lifestyle that some described as \\"convivial\\" while others though \\"excessive\\" a better term. He argued alternatively that a cigarette permanently locked in his hand and the love of a \\"second bottle\\" were as much sources of inspiration for his writing as his limited repertoire of heroes like Paine and Orwell. He knew the source of his problems but that's not the point of this book. It is in essence a slow diary of his journey through \\"\\"Tumortown\\" its excruciating levels of pain, the corresponding fatalism and resignation, its false hopes and eventual knock out blow. There are brilliant passages on figures as diverse as Leonard Cohen, and Nietzsche, a revisiting of the waterboarding torture which Hitchens endured to attack the Bush administration with a about with a searing polemic and finally a weariness at the offerings of possible cancer cures. `You sometimes feel that you may expire from sheer ADVICE\\", he exclaims in frustration<br /><br />This short book concludes with a chapter of fragmentary jottings which are in every sense the most affecting part of the book. The broken phrases and quotes show a mind that thinks deeply, still questioning, still at work and debating until the very last. This is despite of \\"Chemo-brain. Dull, stuporous\\" and fears that this \\"lavish torture is only the prelude to a gruesome execution\\". Hitchens also brilliantly unearths a quote from Saul Bellow which argues with simple insight that \\"death is the dark backing that a mirror needs if we are able to see anything\\". Christopher Eric Hitchens was a man who did his fair share of seeing not least on his many travels to chart despotism and dictatorship and to rally against it with clarity not heard since George Orwell. He also always had the right words even when he was fundamentally wrong and the best of his writings are furiously brilliant, deserving the widest readership whether you agree with him or not. Hitchens died on 15th December 2011, and this the book concludes with a tender \\"Afterword\\" from his widow Carol Blue. At one point in \\"Mortality\\" the author quotes Horace Mann's observation that \\"Until you have done something for humanity you should be ashamed to die\\". In the case of the sadly lamented and much missed Christopher Hitchens there was no need to worry about this, you did more than enough.ann's observation that \\"Until you have done something for humanity you should be ashamed to die\\". In the case of the sadly lamented and much missed Christopher Hitchens there was no need to worry about this, you did more than enough.

B00007GZQZ|4.0|My bargain-bin movie for last night was \\"The Money Pit\\" starring Tom Hanks and Shelly Long. It was kinda funny, but plays on some dreaded fears faced by adult homeowners operating on a limited budget. Let's say you just bought your first home. It was an older model, but you got it for a good price (a steal). Then as you're moving in everything starts going wrong and needs repairs. Well, you know how hard it is to get repairs and how expensive. Then, after long delays, all the various repairmen show up at the same time. Well, almost any homeowner has experienced that sort of thing. Well multiply your experience by an order of magnitude and you have the basic plot of \\"The Money Pit.\\"<br /><br />Hanks and Long play a pair of NYC lovers living in her ex-husband's vacant apartment while he is living in Europe. Well, he unexpectedly shows up so our pair are out house-hunting in the New York City area. They find an out-in-the-country, million-dollar mansion, on emergency sale for only $200K. They can't believe their good fortune and buy it with only a cursory examination. Well, what do you think follows? This mansion starts to fall apart in ways you couldn't even imagine. Everything collapses, goes up in smoke or runs over. It's almost painful for a home owner to watch. And with every little thing, you can see the dollar signs multiplying out of control. It is, indeed, a money pit! Then there are the repairs. Of course, nobody is immediately available and they all need up-front bucks to even think about fixing anything. Meanwhile, our couple is forced to live in their newly-acquired trash heap. Then to cap it all off, about a dozen different repair crews all show up on essentially the same day and the the whole fiasco turns into complete chaos. Naturally, the stress level gets so high that the couple splits, at least temporarily.<br /><br />Now, do you find this to be funny? Well, just look over some of the five-star reviews and some reviewers think this is the funniest thing they've ever seen in their lives. My guess would be that these are apartment dwellers who have never even owned a home or carried a large mortgage. Personally, I got a few good laughs out of the movie, but I wouldn't go so far as to say these people's tragedy was hilarious. That's just not my idea of humor. In any case, the movie was well done and the acting was good, if somewhat painful to watch.<br /><br />Gary Petersoning they've ever seen in their lives. 0375507256|5.0|It's been months since I have written a review.  In truth, I thought I was done with writing reviews on Amazon and Goodreads, but Cloud Atlas is such a special book that I had to come back.<br /><br />The thing that makes Cloud Atlas so incredibly impressive is that is a first-person narrative told through several first persons.  Let me put this another way.  Cloud Atlas is not so much a single novel as it is six novellas, each told from the perspective of a major participant, each period piece from a different moment in history.  What the reader discovers is that all of these stories and voices are as intricately related as the notes of a fine symphony.<br /><br />The book begins with the journal of Adam Ewing, an American who stumbles through a chain of small islands off New Zealand in the 1850s.  Ewing is by all indications, a good man, though perhaps not entirely courageous who lands himself in dangerous straits with the captain and crew of the ship he is traveling on.<br /><br />Ewing does not finish telling his tale of his dangerous voyage, at least not in the first half of the book.  In fact, he stops in mid-sentence.<br /><br />This is where Cloud Atlas is both brilliant and cunningly frustrating.  You see, just as the reader begins to understand just how much trouble Adam Ewing is in, the novel abandons him and takes up the letters of Robert Frobisher, a scoundrel and a fop who has been excommunicated from his fabulously well-to-do family and burned through every penny he owns.<br /><br />Having burned every bridge he has in his own native England, Frobisher leaves for Belgium where he hopes to dupe an aging composer into hiring him as an apprentice.  A talented musician, Frobisher hopes to steal the old goats work and publish it under his own name.<br /><br />Once he has ingratiated himself with the old man, Frobisher begins skulking around the house and sizing up other pilfering.  It is during this time that Frobisher discovers Adam Ewing's journal... well, half of Ewing's journal.  He reads the half that he can find, becomes engrossed, and then to his displeasure, discovers that the second half is missing.<br /><br />Frobisher relates his story through a series of letters to Rufus Sixsmith, whom the reader never meets during Frobisher's time.  Just as Frobisher weaves his way into the sweet old composer's good graces, the reader is whisked away to California, circa 1975, and another story--that of a young reporter names Luisa Rey.<br /><br />Luisa lives in the same building as a very old man named Rufus Sixsmith... yes, the very same Rufus Sixsmith who received long letters from the incorrigible Robert Frobisher.  Luisa is the daughter of a distinguished Vietnam-era reporter.  She would like to write heavy-hitting investigations as well; but, unfortunately she's been relegated to gossip and celebrity-style stories instead.  That changes when Sixsmith, now a famed scientist, tells her about underhanded dealings at a soon-to-open nuclear power plant.<br /><br />While working on the story, Luisa Rey gets her hands on the Frobisher letters, which she finds remarkable.  She does not have a lot of time to read them, however, as she finds herself in over her head on her new story.<br /><br />And here, once again, Cloud Atlas, abandons an excellent storyline and takes up a new one.  In this case, it is the story of Timothy Cavendish, a schmuck of the Robert Frobisher variety who publishes books.  Cavendish Books is a vanity press--a company that publishes the unpublishable for a price.<br /><br />As this story begins, we learn that the aging Cavendish has printed a book called Knuckle Sandwich, which is basically the life story and musings of a London thug.  The book is visceral though not beautifully written.  It might well have gone unnoticed had its author, very much a thug, not thrown the only critic to review the book from a skyscraper during a cocktail party.  Suddenly, the thug is in jail for a very public murder and Knuckle Sandwich is the book on everybody's list.<br /><br />Thinking he is free and clear, Cavendish spends the profits only to discover that the thug has three equally thuggish brothers who want their share.  It is while Cavendish is on the run that he  looks through a titled A Luisa Rey Mystery.<br /><br />And now we jump to the future and Sonmi-451--a clone created in the world of Nea So Copros, which is the Korea of the future.  In Nea So Copros, clones are created to work menial jobs that are seen as beneath the dignity of real people.  Other clones are fed drugs and kept in a in a mental haze, but Sonmi-451 manages to transcend her station, becoming sentient and therefore is perceived as a threat to the social order.<br /><br />By the time the reader meets Sonmi-451, she is captured and scheduled for execution.  We hear her story as she relates it to an \\"archivist\\" who has been assigned to preserve this piece of history.<br /><br />While all of the storylines in Cloud Atlas are strong, this one was probably my favorite.  The Sonmi-451 story is compelling and unique.  It is Flowers For Algernon without the regression.  It is redemptive.  Like A Clockwork Orange, it has its own language; and like Anghony Burgess, Mitchell is able to teach the reader his made-up idiom as the story goes.  Very, very impressive work.<br /><br />The Sonmi-451 story is inter-related to the Cavendish story.  During her ascension, she sees a forbidden \\"disney\\" titled \\"The Ghastly Ordeal of Timothy Cavendish.\\"<br /><br />And the last character on the Cloud Atlas-card, a post-apocalyptic tribesman in Hawaii named Zachry sees Sonmi-451 in a movie as well--a holographic movie very reminiscent of R2D2 showing Han Solo the video of Princess Leia.  Zachry is a young goat herder in dystopian Hawaii, an island with peaceful valley men trying to co-exist with ferocious tribesmen called the \\"Kona.\\"<br /><br />Zachry's life is disrupted when Meronym, a sort of futuristic Jane Goodall, comes to live with him and his family while observing his primitive tribe.  This was my least favor section of the book.  Like Sonmi-451, whom Zachry's tribe worships as a goddess, Zachry speaks in a language that is largely different than English.  It is sort of a patoi of Jamaican lingo and Hawaii's native pidgin.  The story is good and the writing is fine.  Being the least interesting segment in Cloud Atlas still qualifies this story as well worth the read.<br /><br />David Mitchell manages to conjure six fascinating worlds, all well researched and described, all populated by fascinating characters.<br /><br />This book is a remarkable achievement.d Knuckle Sandwich is the book on everybody's list.<br /><br />Thinking he is free and clear, Cavendish spends the profits only to discover that the thug has three equally thuggish brothers who want their share. B0084IG8TM|4.0|It would seem that making a movie based on a successful series of books would be an easy thing but that's not the case. Then there are the fans. When you have a book as popular as THE HUNGER GAMES, those fans can be your best friend or your worst enemy. Everyone has their favorite thing about the book they love and not all will make it to the final product. Fans that have yet to see the movie should know there are changes but that the movie is still wonderful.<br /><br />The story takes place in the future where after a tumultuous war the continent has been divided into districts. Each district is responsible for its own product which for the most part is taken to the Capitol. As a tribute and a reminder of their defeat by those in the Capitol a yearly competition is held called the Hunger Games. A boy and girl, ages 12-18, are selected from each district. They are wined, dined and trained for a few days then set free in an area designated for the games. The way to win is to kill the other 23 contestants.<br /><br />The focus of the story revolves around Katniss Everdeen (Jennifer Lawrence), a young woman who hunts illegally to keep her family fed and taken care of. This year is the first that her young sister Prim must enter her name. The odds are against her name being drawn but when it is, Katniss volunteers to take her place. The boy chosen is Peeta Mellark (Josh Hutcherson), the son of the local baker. The two are allowed their goodbyes to friends and family and then whisked away to the Capitol.<br /><br />On their train trip to the Capitol both Katniss and Peeta are given instruction from their two mentors. Effie Trinket (Elizabeth Banks) is the prim and proper example of what the Capitol is all about: excess in nearly every area and blindly obedient to the President (Donald Sutherland). Their other mentor, clearly the better of the two, is Haymitch Abernathy (Woody Harrelson). Haymitch is a past winner who has knowledge of the games first hand. He also drinks non-stop in an effort to get through the knowledge that perhaps these two might not make it home again.<br /><br />With the story set up we get to the Capitol and witness the transformation of Katniss and Peeta from backwoods teens whose names were drawn to contestants who must not only be physically fit but must win the favor of viewers and wealthy sponsors who can help them with gifts throughout the games. It is their best chance for survival but Katniss hates the games and what they represent. Still, several incidents occur that win her favor in some circles.<br /><br />The day arrives and the contestants are set free to do bodily harm to one another. Backpacks with special equipment are left for them to take if they can avoid being stabbed or worse by other contestants. As the person with the best skills and chance of surviving, Katniss sets out to find a way to win taking as few lives as possible. When the rules are changed to allow both competitors from each district survive as a team, she must find Peeta who proclaimed his love for her during a pre-game interview. Together they might have a chance to make it out of the Hunger Games alive.<br /><br />Movie fans of the film BATTLE ROYALE will raise a stink that this film has taken its idea from that one. These are two different worlds on the same planet and the reactions of Peeta and Katniss show that they've never seen anything outside their front door. It doesn't mean that they admire this new world; they just recognize it is different.<br /><br />The acting is straight forward here. No one seems to be hamming it up or overplaying their roles. Each one comes across as the person they are here to represent which would seem easier for lesser known actors and much more difficult for those who are seasoned professionals. All included in this film turn in admirable jobs.<br /><br />Knowing that the books are a trilogy one could be afraid that the end of this film would leave you hanging on the edge, waiting for the next. Don't be afraid. This movie would stand on its own if it had to. But once you become a fan my guess is you'll anxiously anticipate the next or perhaps you'll begin reading the books. B005F9TQ4C|4.0|This 1978 release may be seen as the book-end of Dylan's great creative wave in the 1970s ushered in with `Planet Waves' and including the seminal BoTT and the good but quirky `Desire' album. SL was Dylan's last `secular' album for some years, the 80s dominated by his exploration of Christianity and his more `white gospel' sound.<br /><br />SL is a generally upbeat album with some sing-along melodies (especially `Baby stop crying' and the opener `Changing of the Guards'), easy to `get' after only a couple of plays and definitive proof that Dylan always could write good tunes, not just great poetic lyrics. Dylan sings throughout the album with great power and gusto, like he really means it.<br /><br />Criticisms of the original 1978 release of SL focussed on the messy-sounding production, excessive use of backing vocals, overbearing intrusion of orchestra and horns and generally poor `finish' of the album's sound. In 1978 these criticisms had merit. However via various remastering efforts over the years, these issues have been much improved and the result is a good, if not quite a great Dylan album which nevertheless sits in the second rank of his epic canon of achievements. Dylan has always worked hard to resist being `pigeon-holed' and in common with his other work from the period, SL has its own unique character and sounds like nothing else he ever recorded.

The book is presented as the first of its kind to have focused on the worrying link between money laundering and developing economies.<br /><br />Specifically, the immense and special difficulties such countries face when tackling money laundering are subjected to detailed scrutiny and analysis and backed by a great deal of pertinent research.<br /><br />The author, Norman Mugarura, suggests certain factors such as, for example, corruption, lack of adequate infrastructure and the failure of systems (systemic failure) in less developed countries as being largely to blame for the proliferation of this type of criminal activity.<br /><br />We assume that `systemic failure' refers to the inefficiency of, or lack of, efficient and/or ethics-based administrative and judicial systems to combat this and similar crime and corruption-related problems. `Unfortunately in some jurisdictions,' admits Mugarura, `the role of the courts is undercut by the obduracy of governments, which treat them as conduits for their wishes.... (and) `mere accessories of the state, primarily interpreting laws to foster the will of parliament.'<br /><br />Yes, this international problem appears multi-faceted and intractable, but the author nevertheless calls for `a global anti-money laundering framework' which, in his view (if we are interpreting this correctly) might well be hampered, or thwarted by a number of challenges related to `the dynamics of the market economy' -- presumably that which exists in developed countries -- such as deregulation, liberalization and conflict of laws.<br /><br />Law certainly meets international financial crime and criminology analysed from a global perspective in this book.<br /><br />Commercial law practitioners and others specializing in comparative law will find it of interest, as will students and academics doing research in this area.  Speaking of research, there is a lengthy and useful bibliography - about six pages long to be precise -- of learned articles, publications and books pertaining to this subject (with titles such as `Dirty Money'), plus useful indexing.<br /><br />The four appendices, also useful, include the deliberations of The Financial Action Task Force (FATF), an intergovernmental body which recommends various anti-money laundering processes, principles and procedures.  The book certainly reflects the copious amount of research and hard work expended by the author and is certainly valuable for that. 0385334206|4.0|Kurt Vonnegut offered \\"Breakfast of Champions\\" to himself as a fiftieth birthday present, a way to say \\"Goodbye, Blue Monday\\" to his obvious issues with own depression and his mother's suicide. Viewed uncharitably, BoC might be seen as the author treating his reader as therapist, spewing whatever invective comes into his mind onto the page. But the reason this is more interesting than your average midlife crisis is because, not only is Vonnegut hilarious, but Vonnegut's crisis represents that of the country, which in 1973 finds itself embroiled in a deep and seemingly intractable crisis of capitalism, the promises of post-war growth so clearly spent, with the results of communities torn asunder, environmental devastation, racial strife, and imperialist imbroglio. Growing up in the depression (\\"so many Americans believed then: that the nation would be happy and just and rational when prosperity came\\"), coming of age during the World War (in which he would see an even more unjust and irrational side than most), Vonnegut must settle his accounts not only with his parents but with the false promises of American ideology - and does so right off the bat in the irreverent first chapter.<br /><br />Despite the \\"evil nonsense which children were taught\\", the country with the \\"balderdash\\" national anthem was founded by \\"sea pirates\\" who \\"used human beings for machinery\\" and \\"disciplined other countries by threatening to shoot big rockets on them\\". And consistent with the growing concerns of the early 1970s, the industrial destruction of the land is a major theme in BoC. Vonnegut's alter-ego and creation Kilgore Trout surveys the damage as he hitchhikes in an olive truck past New Jersey sludge and West Virginia strip-mining. In Trout's science fiction, alien visitors note that the \\"every form of life on the once peaceful and moist and nourishing blue-green ball was dying or dead\\". Of course, Trout has long since given up: \\"There's a river in Cleveland which is so polluted it catches fire about once a year. That used to make me sick, but I laugh about it now.\\" Gilgongo! As it's not just the landscape that's bleak, Trout sees himself (and thus does Vonnegut) as \\"a representative of all the thousands of artists who devoted their entire lives to a search for truth and beauty - and didn't find doodley-squat!\\"<br /><br />Instead, Vonnegut scribbles a charcoal of a world where \\"human beings are robots, are machines\\". He looks back on the syphilitics he used to see in Depression Indianapolis \\"vertebrae welded together after the corkscrews got through with the meat between\\". To Vonnegut in crisis, we are all symphilitics, defective machinery; \\"machines made out of meat\\", \\"black machines\\", \\"agreeing machines\\", \\"defective child-bearing machine\\", \\"disappearing machine\\". Machines driven deterministically by \\"bad chemicals\\". And since humans are machines, we are acquainted with minute details of every orifice and sex act that they like to perform. This is the early 1970s after all, and America and Kilgore Trout are getting acquanted with Times Square smut houses.<br /><br />Of course, while Dwayne Hoover learns to think humans are machines from Kilgore Trout (their meeting is the plot anchor of the book), Vonnegut doesn't actually think so - or at least he is conflicted. There is a strip of day-glo orange tape running down his dark canvas. That is the abstract art of Rabo Karabekian, representing \\"the immaterial core of every animal... it is unwavering and pure, no matter what preposterous adventure may befall us.\\"<br /><br />Through Vonnegut though, the \\"unwavering and pure\\" side of the human spirit doesn't seem all that convincing. It's more something he must believe in, to keep the bad chemicals from forcing him to succumb to the same fate as his mother, Dwayne Hoover's wife, etc.
0754677281|5.0|[[VIDEOID:mo2AIODRV5E9RJC]]<br /><br />THE RULE AND ROLE OF LAW IN THE DEVELOPING WORLD<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />Recently published by Ashgate, `Legitimacy, Legal Development and Change' presents a collection of articles and essays which set out to explore, analyse and define the often complex and wide-ranging area of what we would call comparative jurisprudence; in this case legal development in the developing world.<br /><br />The book brings together the work of twenty-six academics and scholars from top universities and institutions worldwide. In the words of editor David K. Linnan, the book `explores overlapping themes, asking how legal development and change actually works and focusing on specific examples of legal systems, mainly in non-Western jurisdictions in Asia, Africa, the Islamic world and additionally, South America.<br /><br />The book in our view references one of Jurgen Habermas's thesis, developed in the 1980s on what was termed the `legitimation crisis', which basically asks the question, `who sets the political agenda?  Is it Parliament, (comprising the government [executive] and legislature)? Or is it the legal establishment, including lawyers and the judiciary?  Or is it social change which is eventually legitimized by law? Or is it law which dictates social change?<br /><br />In common law jurisdictions, the common law often makes the cases and establishes precedents.  This constitutes development largely by evolution rather than revolution, of law and legal systems, although there have been some quite explosive revolutions along the way in common law countries, with people fighting for their rights based on a particular value system which places freedom at the top of the collective wish list.<br /><br />Bearing all this in mind, this book is interesting in that it is a jurisprudential collection which points the way forward toward legal philosophical thinking from a global perspective, now and in the future.<br /><br />The learned articles are grouped under five categories, from `Changing the ROL Narrative' (Rule of Law, in case you didn't know.) to `International Law as Legal Development Subject', which includes an article on the European Court of Human Rights and the Diversity of Legal Cultures.  Parts II and III centre on religious law, particularly The Qur'anic Perspective - (and thank goodness there are a couple of women's rights articles included).  Japan and Russia are scrutinized in Parts IV and VI.<br /><br />The various contributors, all with impressive academic and professional credentials, each appear to discuss the multiplicity of issues involved from an entirely objective, empirical viewpoint without any obvious political or partisan bias.<br /><br />In striving for complete objectivity, however, some cannot fail to betray either a pro-Western or anti-Western mindset, which is, of course, understandable. Says the editor:  `given the presence of non-Western alongside Western models for legal development, whose modernization concept becomes the operative question.'  Boy, it sure does become `the operative question,' we would add -- and the choice is crucial.<br /><br />Developing countries generally, do not have centuries of legal evolution to fall back on as a paradigm, so what choice will they make toward their own recently evolving, i.e. `modernizing' legal systems?  Will they indeed `modernize', or regress?  The future welfare of millions depends on the answer, or answers -- and this book certainly provides an important contribution to this vital debate.from a global perspective, now and in the future.<br /><br />The learned articles are grouped under five categories, from `Changing the ROL Narrative' (Rule of Law, in case you didn't know.) to `International Law as Legal Development Subject', which includes an article on the European Court of Human Rights and the Diversity of Legal Cultures. B008VNIA32|5.0|I just want to chime in to thank Kultur for releasing my favorite Ring Cycle in video in the blu-ray format.(*) Compared with [[ASIN:B005FWO3QK Kultur's previous DVD release]] of the same performance, this blu-ray release has slight enhancement in the video and in the DTS 5.1 audio track, but with no perceptible difference in the LPCM audio track. Here are some points which might prove useful for your purchase decision.<br /><br />0. This Ring Cycle was filmed in the summer of 1991 and 1992 at the Bayreuth Festspielhaus without the audience, after a 5-year period of Bayreuth Workshop. It is the first Ring Cycle recorded in the true surround setting. Perhaps due to the 5-year experience, the performers have the wonderful sense of \\"living-in\\" the drama.<br /><br />1. If my personal taste is any guide, this is by very far the best musical performance of the ring cycle ever recorded *in video* to date. Daniel Barenboim proves himself a true Wagner conductor (and a true disciple of Wilhelm Furtwngler) with this magnificently intense performance, probably the best in decades. Here every detail in the score contributes to the drama and no dramatic event is not underlined by the music. He also picked many fresh voices over the established Wagnerian singers. For example neither Anne Evans nor John Tomlinson is your average Wagner singer. Several of the principals had never performed their roles before, including Tomlinson, Jerusalem, and von Kannen. They however deliver their roles with fresh voice and insight. (It's worth noting that Evans and many others in this production had been trained by Reginald Goodall in the [[ASIN:B00005LZVY English National Opera's Ring production]] and it shows.) On the theatrical front, Harry Kupfer's probing production can probably share the pantheon with [[ASIN:B0009F2EPU Bayreuth Centennial Ring production by Patrice Chreau]]. At least, these two are my favorite staging of the Ring, with [[ASIN:B0019LZ19O The Copenhagen Ring]] running adistant third. On the surface, Kupfer's unconventional characterization of the characters can seem extreme. However, the inner logic quickly reveals itself (especially on the repeat viewing) and the extremism becomes inevitability. Wagner's music thus reaches its fullest dramatic potential.<br /><br />2. The previous [[ASIN:B005FWO3QK Kultur DVD release]] has greatly improved the video and audio quality over the preceding Warner Classics releases in [[ASIN:B000QFBW6K Der Ring Des Nibelungen (7 DVD)]] and [[ASIN:B002G1TSBI Ring Des Nibelungen (7 DVD)]]. Now with the blu-ray release the video and the DTS sound track  see some further improvements. I have had mixed feeling about Kultur. On the one hand, I am thankful that the company keeps a lot of music films in print at affordable prices. On the other hand, their DVD post-production work is often subpar. Too often, the company chooses low bitrate processing/encoding to fit the film into less DVD discs. As a  result, grainy video with distorted colors and heavily compressed poor audio quality are almost synonymous to Kultur. There, however, Kultur surprises us with this very high quality work. According to the booklet, both video and the audio are remastered from the original high definition master tapes. In order to maximize the video/audio quality of this new presentation Kultur decides to spread the opera into 11 DVD discs, with highest bitrates. The improvements in both departments are nothing less than spectacular. Now the video is crystal clear and the sound has stunning depth, both in LPCM and in DTS 5.1 tracks. Even if you own the previous Warner release, the upgrade is well worth it.<br /><br />3. This blu-ray edition is an ALL REGION release. So go ahead and purchase it even if you don't live in Northern America.<br /><br />4. It is worth repeating that even if the resolution of video and sound is identical to the previous [[ASIN:B005FWO3QK Kultur's DVD edition]], it is just so much more convenient to have one disc per opera/night. Save viewers the hassle of constantly changing discs! Now, with the improved video and audio, the choice is obvious for those who own a blu-ray player.<br /><br />5. However, this is an early HiFi video recording and there is infrequent streaking in the dark black area. The multi-channel setting also places emphasis on the two main speakers. In terms of technical aspects, you will not mistake this as a new recording. The best reason to buy this set is for its artistic merits.<br /><br />Most highly recommended.<br />----------------<br />(*) I was among the first group to have purchased Kultur's release of remastered Barenboim's Bayreuth Ring cycle. Initially, Kultur goofed up in the first release. The LPCM sound track on the second half of Disc 8 was in mono. (That is, Siegfried Act III, Track 11 onward, right after Siegfried's \\"Das ist kein Mann!\\".) Fortunately, Kultur owns up the mistake: Not only was the problem quickly fixed once the company was notified, but they also sent out free replacement discs when contacted. (I got my replacement copy promptly.)<br /><br />While corresponding with Kultur's \\"John\\", I urged the company to release it in the blu-ray format. \\"Even if the resolution of video and sound can not be further improved, it is just so much more convenient to have one disc per opera/night!\\" And I pledged to purchase the blu-ray version once it is released. (As you can see, I pre-ordered the set!) So consider this review a thank-you note to Kultur.

1451683405|3.0|The insight doing all the philosophical work here is this: you can't pinpoint the discrete fraction of a second where conscious intention causes something that neuronal billiard balls do not. The empirical back-up are several experiments that show (A) tell-tale firings in the brain slightly before a person thinks he or she has made some kind of choice, and (B) certain techniques (think hypnosis) that can allow a researcher to influence a choice that the subject believes he or she made independently. The armchair version of all this is the thought experiment in which you try to identify introspectively where YOU rather than your brain decides, say, to move your left hand rather than your right. From this evidence, the annihilation of freedom is proclaimed.<br /><br />As a reader, I experienced all this as rather underwhelming, despite Harris's undeniable essayistic flare. The first problem in my mind (free or not!) is that the messy relation between the \\"inside\\" and \\"outside\\" perspective on freedom as demonstrated by such experiments is hardly a basis for making some kind of conclusive judgment, in the infancy of neuroscience, about minds and causation. (See [[ASIN:0195399692 The Oxford Handbook of Free Will: Second Edition]] for philosophical reservations about this data that a serious study should address with more than dismissive name-calling, of which Harris is occasionally guilty.) In his zeal, Harris may demonstrate a failure of imagination here. If I understand him correctly, there is mechanistic-materialist causation and a hard-dualist mental causation and nothing important in between. I think this cannot be right. Aesthetically, it is too simple a solution to such a complex problem. There are too many terms involved whose philosophical meaning is not obvious. [[ASIN:0393049914 Terrence Deacon]], for one, has muddied these waters.<br /><br />Or is Harris talking about some kind of non-materialist, non-reductionist determinism? If so, I would ask if such a thing even makes sense as a concept. What is doing the determining? In the name of what, exactly, is freedom pronounced dead?<br /><br />In any case, false dichotomies are not foreign to Harris's thinking. His past discussions of religion, for instance, contain various question-begging ahistorical simplifications of actual religious thought, experience, and practice, which hardly give his reader a fair choice of viewpoints. (Scott Atran's evidence-based rejection of Harris's sociologically simplistic religion-terrorism insight is relevant here. I watched Harris give a very lame response to Atran on this point.) In the current text, his pairing of the free will doctrine and religion is something of a howler, since the entire complex of Christian doctrines surrounding original sin and free grace suggest (poetically, if you will) exactly what Harris is proclaiming. But I digress. At the very least, Harris does seem to believe, unlike the eliminative materialists, in the reality of consciousness, but only as a 100% passive after-effect of neuronal activity. It is a real ghost, but a pointless and powerless one schlepped along by the engine of the brain.<br /><br />The second problem concerns Harris's attitude towards moral responsibility. He makes the correct observation that moral thinking is too natural and valuable to reject out of hand, but I don't think he _personally_ can buy into talk of moral responsibility, since moral responsibility can hardly be a coherent concept to one has lifted the veil of Maya and perceived the illusion of freedom. He asserts that this insight makes him more forgiving and compassionate (hardly qualities that most people would associate with his passion for cultural genocide), although these qualities cannot be to his credit, as there can be no \\"credit\\" where there is no free moral agent. The enlightened have no choice, it seems to me, but to understand this, and the whole question of value--whether realist, subjectivist, or transcendent--loses its importance. I can see no way, in short, for Harris to counter indifference, cynicism, selfishness, or depression, unless it is to say: \\"But look at me! I'm America and you can too!\\" But if someone (if there are someones) doesn't care to score cultural-political points for the congregation of the righteous? What then? Can you (if there are yous) simply ignore the irony of it all? I personally could not. I would find everything too stupid for words.<br /><br />The enigma of selfhood is a tremendous thing to ponder. It is certainly true that the full moral, cognitive, and metaphysical meaning of the \\"I\\" is mysterious and potentially unsettling. Also, only by understanding the deep embeddedness of consciousness in a biological framework can the wonder and the constraints of sentience and moral consciousness first come to light. At points Harris takes our thoughts in such directions, but in the end I feel that he trivializes the whole shebang for the sake of ideological closure. And, I'm guessing, a cheer from some choir or other.

No relationship is happy go lucky or perfect, nor is it Edward and Bella(even they were crazy)...sometimes relationships are volatile and disastrous, which is exactly what this is. However, it shows that even in the midst of all they put each other through,they realize that they need each other and that together they work well. No, they do not physically abuse each other but in an effort to make each other happy they hurt each other emotionally. I feel that if they just talked about things they would've saved themselves some trouble.<br /><br />We as a society are attracted to chaos. Look at reality TV as a prime example. Though we know its pure insanity, some of us get sucked in because we want to know how it ends. Beautiful Disaster is an intense mess, but the love is beautiful. For me, this book is about the ability to live the experience of how it feels to be in this type of relationship. The writing is realistic and it felt like I was inside the book as events began to unfold. I look forward to getting into Travis's head in the next book. Overall, an interesting read that I recommend to a mature audience.<br /><br />I received a complimentary copy of the book in exchange for an honest review.

B0000667RO|3.0|A compilation is supposed to represent an artist's best and most well-known material, right? Well in that case, the title is misleading.<br /><br />The problem for me is actually the decision to include two of his old time rockers. By putting \\"Run to You\\" and \\"Summer of '69\\" in here for good measure, it suddenly puts the rest of the album in perspective, because they just happen to be the album's best songs.<br /><br />I know now that the album was supposed to contain his 90's hits and that it's actually named after the title track. But back then, neither me nor my dad knew it.<br /><br />When my dad found this disc in our local record store, he hadn't listened to Bryan Adams since he was a teenager, when he was a big fan of his. He had therefore forgotten a lot of the songs he loved back then, and was vastly disappointed when he put this disc on, only to hear flutes and violins, then recognize five songs and hear almost no rockers, or even \\"Heaven\\". These were not the songs he wanted to hear.<br /><br />That's why I don't like this collection. It's not called \\"The Best of the 90's\\" or \\"The Best of the Ballads\\", it's called \\"The Best of Me\\", as if this was a career-covering disc. It's not, and you can't expect casual fans to know that \\"The Best of Me\\" is merely a reference to a new song.<br /><br />You might ask why we didn't simply buy \\"So Far So Good\\" instead. Simple, this was the newest collection and all the stores sold this one, and we actually didn't know it existed, since I had barely heard any Bryan Adams songs at the time and my dad lost interest after \\"Waking Up the Neighbours\\".<br /><br />I more or less like all of these songs by themselves, but not when it's shoddily packaged together under an album title like this one. If they would have traded \\"Run to You\\" and \\"Summer of '69\\" in favour of \\"On a Day Like Today\\" and \\"Star\\" and add \\"There Will Never Be Another Tonight\\", \\"Tonight I'd Died and Gone to Heaven\\", \\"Fearless\\" and \\"Blues Jam\\", it would have beena great 90's compilation. Instead, it's just average.<br /><br />It's okay now, since we've now got \\"Anthology\\" and I'm more familiar with his discography. But back then, it was a disappointment, and since disc 2 of [the international release of] \\"Anthology\\" is just a little bit better than \\"The Best of Me\\", there is no real reason to buy this record, since you can buy the hidden track \\"Don't Give Up\\" by itself. (Unless you really want a blurry photo of Bryan Adams standing naked, that is, since \\"Anthology\\" doesn't have such a picture...)

B008K7WCJM|5.0|I'm going to start this right off by saying that I now fully believe Swans mastermind Michael Gira's statement that 'The Seer' took 30 years to make and is \\"the culmination of every previous Swans album and any other music I've made.\\" At first I thought he was being hyperbolic, but after two straight listens and four hours gone by, I'm pretty much exhausted and drained--physically and emotionally--like I'd just run the gamut of every conceivable emotion, including two or three that I never knew existed. Try \\"scared\\" yet \\"blissful.\\" Or \\"sad\\" yet \\"euphoric,\\" which seems like it makes no sense, but I can almost guarantee that if you listen with an open mind (for those of you who may be new to Swans) and just let the music take over, it will.<br /><br />And, like Gira, I'm not exaggerating. While I'm definitely a fan of a lot of his past work, I'm not a hardcore Swans fan who's predisposed to think everything he puts out is genius. In truth, I have to be in just the right mood for his music, and even then there's only so much I can take before I feel like lying down in the middle of a busy expressway, due to suddenly feeling yet another unique-to-Swans-emotion that goes a little something like this: \\"I'm an insignificant speck in this vast universe, so what's the point,\\" mixed with \\"horrified\\" and something a little like vertigo. Of course, even creating music that induces nihilism and dizziness takes tremendous talent, but still, it can wear on you.<br /><br />'The Seer,' though, is much more sprawling in its scope than even 1996's epic 'Soundtracks for the Blind,' both in its emotional content and its virtual myriad of musical styles. Sure, you'll find \\"depressing\\" here. Quite a bit, in fact. But then there are moments of nearly overwhelming beauty to counteract it, making the highs that much higher. While this album is instantly recognizable as being the product of Swans, it definitely breaks new musical ground while still nodding to the band's--and at times music ingeneral's--past.<br /><br />I'm not going to break the album down track by track, as it needs to be experienced fully in order to get the maximum effect. Suffice to say, you'll hear repetitious, skull-piercing riffs and drones that slowly morph into something else over time; ambient--yet tension-filled--Brian Eno-esque innerspace soundscapes; jazzy, almost Krautrockian post-rock; intense tribal rhythms; even a country-ish ballad in the Karen-O sung \\"Song for a Warrior.\\" Not to mention what may be the most beautiful, awe-inspiring song of Swans' career: the 19-minute \\"A Piece of the Sky,\\" featuring former member Jarboe's ethereal vocals.<br /><br />Gira's voice is in full-effect throughout the album: from near-angelic, to ominous, to straight-up primal, and everything in-between, with lyrics that can still be devastating, especially if one's in the right frame of mind. Throw in some cameos by Ben Frost, the husband and wife duo of Alan Sparhawk & Mimi Parker from Low, and the aforementioned Karen-O, and what you have is possibly the most ambitious, demanding, most exciting album of the year, in any genre.<br /><br />It's amazing to me that, at this late stage in his 30 year career, Gira's still getting better; musically, vocally, and lyrically. It's a rare thing indeed for an artist to still be peaking at 58 years-old. But 'The Seer' is, without a doubt, a work of art, and the best album of his career, imo. Maybe, in another couple years, this will be considered just his second-best, though it's hard to imagine anything topping this. But at this rate, it wouldn't surprise me if he keeps getting better for yet another 30 years. All I know is, we're all lucky to have him.

B00123IATW|5.0|I'm going to keep this one brief because the album has already been discussed in detail by the many other fine reviewers on Amazon. I do have a point I'd like to make that I believe will influence your buying decision (assuming you are one of the few who doesn't already own this classic).<br /><br />The highs and lows of Yes...and prog rock in general...the mind-numbing precision and stomach-churning excess...can all be found RIGHT HERE.<br /><br />The STUDIO VERSIONS of the songs on this album are infinitely superior to ANY versions the band released in live formats later. Why? Because they felt that instead of the TIGHT and AMAZING versions here, the songs had to be used as a platform to \\"jam\\" (witness the wretched \\"guitar face\\" version of \\"Starship Trooper\\" from the Trevor Rabin edition of the band on \\"9012Live: The Solos,\\" where he prances around the stage like Nigel Tufnel and bassist Chris Squire attempts to stalk him like a cross between the Wolfman and Gene Simmons...talk about a performance that has NOT worn well over the decades)...<br /><br />...and the famous coda from that song, \\"Wurm,\\" is where it all becomes apparent.<br /><br />1). @ the 5:36 mark, Steve Howe introduces that mighty \\"Wurm\\" chord riff.<br /><br />2). Around the 6:00 mark you can hear Tony Kaye building a little keyboard swell behind him. Note that I didn;t say \\"Rick Wakeman starts spewing this noodly tweedly rambling little solo to blot Howe out.\\"<br /><br />3). Around 6:30, the precise, booming anchor of Chris Squire becomes increasingly prominent. Bruford is also VERY slowly working his way in. We also get introduced to this fuzzy, distorted riff that is STILL FOLLOWING THE MAIN RIFF.<br /><br />4). Around the 7 minute mark, Bruford is opening up, as is Kaye. B0075LTOR2|5.0|Although they never achieved the fame in the U.S. that they did in the U.K., Roxy Music remained an influential band with their art-rock/glam-rock approach. The band's first five studio albums are seminal while their last three studio albums range from decent to exceptional. This handsome boxed set collects the band's eight studio albums in handsome, thick, glossy gatefold mini-LP sleeves along with two CDs of the band's various b-sides, singles and various remixes. Although this set is missing previously unreleased tracks or outtakes, there are a number of releases on the last two CD set that make their debut in digital form for the first time.<br /><br />Although fans may argue over the merit of various releases (was the band better with or without Eno? Did Eddie Jobson's musical sophistication as a keyboard and violin player expand the band's musical sound), these are all worthwhile picking up for fans in this new remastering which goes back to the original analog sources. Reportedly these are \\"flat\\" transfers that represent the sound of the mastertapes more accurately than any other release and while I can't attest to how these compare to the master tapes, these are probably the BEST representation of how the band sounded.<br /><br />These releases sound exceptionally good--they are not brick walled,  not peak limited, compressed nor has there been any attempt to fatten up the bass sound or the introduction of any strange EQ choices. The albums are presented as intended (and, in the case of \\"Manifesto\\" features the original album line up--the original CD release included the single version of \\"Angel Eyes\\" and a different take of \\"Dance Away\\" that were substituted for the original tracks) IMHO these best the UK original E.G. releases as well as the Warner/Reprise CD reissues AND Bob Ludwig's remasters from 1999. The sound is warm and analog-like (which is ironic in our digital age given these albums were produced without ever thinking about how they would sound digitally). The transfers are top notch throughout.  What we get here is as close to the original sound as the band intended for these releases. The labels feature the original album cover graphics on the CD labels.<br /><br />Is this set perfect? For fans who want a booklet detailing the recording of the various albums along with lyrics and details on the mastering of this edition the answer would be no. Also, the original release was to include DVD higher resolution releases of ALL the band's albums and b-sides. This was scaled back to this set here and, while those things are missed, as mentioned these are the ultimate redbook CD editons of the albums.<br /><br />Fans looking for accurate reproductions of the LP sleeves including the inner lining should be aware that these are ALL gatefold sleeves (only the first three were released on LP in gatefold sleeves)and the inner sleeves are color coordinated to the release but blank (\\"Country Life\\", \\"Flesh + Blood\\" and \\"Avalon\\" all included the original lyrics for each release on the inner sleeve something not reproduced here). The gatefolds do have outtake photos from the cover sessions included on the inside (some of which appeared on the boxed set [[ASIN:B0000073GP Thrill of It All]]).<br /><br />The CD of outtakes, b-sides and remixes include the following tracks (collecting everything that the band released during their lifetime--The version of \\"India\\" included on \\"The Thrill of It All\\" on the fourth disc is not a unique mix but the album version and is not included here as a result):<br /><br />Virginia Plain 3:00 (This was not on the original LP release in the UK but a stand alone single)<br Application's Failed  4:45<br />The Thrill of It All [Edit] 4:22<br />The Thrill of It All[USA 7\\" Mix]  3:25<br />Love Is the Drug [USA 7\\"Mix] 3:00<br />Sultanesque  5:26<br />Both Ends Burning 0345803485|3.0|I am a 16 year old that enjoys classic novels and to a lesser extent, historical fiction and romance. However, I am always willing to read popular works, regardless of author or genre. As with many people my age, I disliked Twilight and went through an \\"OMG the Hunger Games is so awesome!!\\" phase. Popular works have always been something I go for just to see if they are really worthy of the hype they receive. Then I read 50 Shades of Grey. It may sound extremely dim-witted but I hardly knew anything about it when I picked it up. It had a sleek cover, and a blurb that sounded like the ultimate dark love fantasy. The ambiguous winky faces that so often came up on Facebook/Twitter whenever this work was discussed only piqued my curiosity. I should have known better...I suppose.<br /><br />The simple style of writing assured me it would be an easy read, the undeniable attraction and tension between Christian and Anastasia drew me in. Then Christian opened the door to his `playroom'. However, once I absorbed this (my typical adolescent curiosity driving me to finish at least the first book in this trilogy.) it was far less troubling. As the book progressed, I found it to have a pretty engrossing plot about a girl torn between a `normal' life she always expected to lead and an unconventional, bizarre lifestyle that she did not even know existed. The twisted, tormented romance between Ana and Christian was delightfully dark and intriguing.<br /><br />Admittedly, Anastasia is a character I liked very little and the repetitive writing style leaves a lot to be desired. Grammatical mistakes are ever present and there are only so many times one can read the same line over and over before it loses all effect and meaning. A Thesaurus would have been a very useful investment. For an author, James's vocabulary does not appear to be very expansive. I mean, `sardonic' is a pretty good word but the amount of times it's used to describe Christian's smile is positively maddening!  And why, oh why does Anastasia have to bite her lip that much. It's almost like a weird pesky habit.  Also, there were pretty blatant inconsistencies (for want of a better word) in the general plot. That Christian is a self made billionaire even before he had reached his 30's I can sort of accept as not being 100% outside the realms of possibility (I mean it is fiction, right?)  but in the book, his job is almost never mentioned. Christian makes being a CEO of a giant company look like a regular 9-5 job...heck, not even that. If running a (relatively new, I am assuming) billion dollar company consists of just taking the occasional phone call then I really see why it's such a dream job.  I will not go into much more detail, but it's mainly these flaws in characterisation, style and basic grammar, not the sex, that makes this book no better than three stars in my opinion.<br />The sex is excessive, but to my surprise it was perfectly integral to the story. It weaves almost seamlessly into the plot, so much so that it made me believe that the effect would be the same without it. Some people's complaints about the book having `so much sex' and being `so dirty' and `disturbing' are pretty unfounded considering it's a work of erotic fiction. And along with that, it is about BDSM; for a popular, bestselling work that is a rare, risky topic. But 50 Shades of Grey without the sex would be like a crime novel without anyone actually committing any criminal acts, it would simply not work. From a personal point of view, I did not enjoy reading the scenes with overly unusual acts, they unnerved me at times. This however was from lack of preparation and knowledge and not from some kind of artistic flaw in the work.<br /><br />In conclusion, 50 Shades of Grey is far from an ideal, delightful little novel that can be enjoyed by people of all ages. However, that was never its purpose and just because it has become popular does not mean it is for absolutely everyone. It deserves praise forits good features just as much as contempt for its faulty writing and other flaws aforementioned. So I decided not to go one way or the other and settled for the comfortable, median rating of 3/5 stars. What I learned (aside from the ...ahem... the obvious details about BDSM that I really did not need to know) is that I should do my research before jumping on the bandwagon because sometimes it can lead me in some very peculiar directions.  Personally, as a whole this book wasn't really for me, even though I finished it.... And had I known more I doubt I would have picked it up in the first place. That's pretty much it, not the most authoritative opinion but I hope a clearly expressed one.

That is how I was introduced to the novels, and actually watched the series on TV before starting in on the first book.  In my opinion they both the TV series and the novels are excellent, and are written in a very different style than your typical fantasy epic.  The characters are gritty and their intentions are not necessarily good or evil, but like real people depend greatly upon the psychology and history of the individual.  The Starks and the Lannisters, two powerful houses are essentially the primary adversaries in this first book, although there are many other houses which complicate matters in the realm.  Above them all the King of Westeros tries to maintain his hold on all seven kingdoms.  This may all sound like typical fantasy writing, but you will soon realize that things are very different in this new world.  Martin writes in a unique style as well, at least one that I have not seen very often.  Each chapter is written from the viewpoint of a certain character, there are maybe 5 or 6 in \\"Game of Thrones\\", and we see the plot unfold from the eyes of various people throughout the world.  Each chapter title is the name of whichever character we are following, probably to make it easier as the number of viewpoints increases.  I found both the books and TV show to be captivating and quite interesting, and I cannot wait for the next season to come out in the fall.  There are many twists and turns, many of them completely unexpected by the reader, and be forewarned, no character is safe from death, even those who seem to be indispensable.  I highly recommend this book to anyone who is a fan of the genre, and even those who aren't will likely enjoy this new twist on the fantasy novel.

0062183591|3.0|Dan Ariely entertains while teaching. That's not such a bad thing. But I like more substance and less fluff. Cotton candy tastes good, but meat and potatoes makes a better meal.<br /><br />In his book The Honest Truth about Dishonesty, Dan Ariely describes his research and tells stories (some about himself, and some about others) focusing on honesty. While entertaining enough, there is little to chew on here. Dan Ariely tries to connect his research to bigger themes like the fraud at Enron, the staggering thefts of Bernie Madoff, and the across-the-board dishonesty shown in the financial meltdown of 2008.<br /><br />But the connection seems fragile. Dan Ariely's research gets nowhere near the mindset and motives of corporate pirates. He focuses instead on the trivial--things like stealing cans of Coke from dorm refrigerators, wearing fake \\"high-fashion\\" sunglasses, and cheating when working with matrices in a contrived experimental study. Not the same at all.<br /><br />Because of that, I had a hard time getting through the book. The first third I read fairly rigorously, the second third I read rather quickly, and the last third I skimmed. I don't think I missed much. As another reviewer said, this book would have been better as an article rather than a book.<br /><br />Much better on a similar topic was the book [[ASIN:1594203385 The Hour Between Dog and Wolf: Risk Taking, Gut Feelings and the Biology of Boom and Bust]]. The author of that book, John Coates, does not have the academic credentials of Dan Ariely. Nor does he have the comedic bent. But where Dan Ariely serves up cotton candy, John Coates serves up meat and potatoes. Although The Hour Between Dog and Wolf has its faults (including a title that makes little sense), it's a much, much better book than this one.<br /><br />One final note about a delicious irony: One of the blurbs on the back of The Honest Truth about Dishonesty reads as follows:<br /><br />In this endlessly fascinating book, Dan Ariely proves that dishonesty is everywhere: we are all bad apples. 145162607X|3.0|While I can still only muster three stars for Gregory's latest installment of her series featuring prominent women from England's Wars of the Roses, it was nevertheless better than her previous efforts.  Based on her mediocre other novels (I enjoyed The Other Boleyn Girl, but never recaptured enjoyment of her books after that), I wouldn't have even bothered reading this one had it not featured Richard Neville's daughter, Anne, a woman who particularly fascinates me.<br /><br />Anne serves as our narrator throughout the story, but Gregory put herself in a tough position here:  Anne is really far too young to ever be a compelling narrator.  The events she must convey to the reader in order for the story to make any sense are not things a young, noble girl would have known.  Therefore, Gregory is forced to make this information known to Anne (so she can tell you, the reader) through servant's gossip or even her father's confidential conversation -- this latter is so highly improbably (read: impossible) that it makes for laugh-out-loud moments throughout the book.  Really, Neville - the Kingmaker himself - would have confided to his pre-teen daughter his battle plans?<br /><br />Additionally, Gregory puts herself in a fix trying to convey a pre-teen's \\"voice\\" in the narrative...the result is what might be a decent Young Adult story, but not good historical fiction for adults.  Good characters are, of course, very, very good while bad characters have horns and tails.  Delicate characterization doesn't really exist.<br /><br />I'm not one to quibble too much with the historical liberties she takes throughout the novel....yes, it's fiction so she's entitled.  With a little more editing/polishing, the novel has potential.  I particularly liked a childbirth scene and her attempt to cast ol' Richard III in a ambivalent light.  Take away Anne as the narrator and she could have put the reader right in the midst of the action, instead of dwelling on what Anne was wearing that particular day and overhearing the entire timetable of the Wars of the Roses via servant's gossip and she might have had a very good novel.  As it stands, this is historical fiction \\"lite\\".  Nothing worth shelling out $20 on, but might be worth a library checkout if you want something fluffy to read while at the doctor/dentist's office.rticular day and overhearing the entire timetable of the Wars of the Roses via servant's gossip and she might have had a very good novel. 0805095535|4.0|From Paul Auster:<br />\\"Perhaps it is something as simple as this: that a man fears death more at fifty-seven than he does at seventy-four.\\"<br /><br />I've only read Paul Auster's nonfiction but I love it -- whether it's life's coincidences in [[ASIN:0811214982 The Red Notebook]] or this memoir on aging, written over a winter as Auster moves from midlife toward old age. I also feel that the total of an Auster book is less than the sum of its parts. In other words: I love the reading but like the overall works somewhat less.<br /><br />Here he journals about his first 64 years and filters his recollections through his body and the spaces around and within it. It's a chapter-less sequence of musings with just the occasional space on a page to separate vignettes. It's written entirely in second-person point of view -- which immediately raised my guard and then surprised me by becoming less visible and even pulling me into the experience just like it's supposed to. The best passages are the sections of 8-10 pages of solid, unparagraphed text, where he dives deeper and takes the reader along; I grew to love seeing them ahead.<br /><br />Whereas Auster wrote about his father in [[ASIN:0143112228 The Invention of Solitude]], here he writes about his mother, including this passage from a night after they visited her gravely ill second husband in the hospital:<br /><br />\\"...just when you thought it would be impossible for anyone to say another word, when the heaviness in your hearts seemed to have crushed all the words out of you, your mother started telling jokes [...] jokes so funny that you and your wife laughed until you could hardly breathe anymore [...] an unending torrent of classic yenta routines with all the appropriate voices and accents, the old Jewish women sitting around a card table and sighing, each one sighing in turn, each one sighing more loudly than the last, until one of the women finally says, 'I thought we agreed not to talk about the children'.\\"<br /><br />And then this:<br />\\"You have seen several corpses in the past {...} but none of those corpses belonged to your mother, no other dead body was the body in which your own life began, and you can look for no more than a few seconds before you turn your head away.\\"<br /><br />As a whole, it feels like a journal -- a notebook filled with stream-of-consciousness writing from prompts in a memoir class (especially the 53 pages -- one-fourth of the book -- that recall the place of his birth and his 21 residential addresses since). Yet the pages are so good! I'm definitely going to read the rest of his nonfiction.

1476712042|2.0|Beautiful Disaster is one of those books that it seems everyone either loves or hates. I am sad to say that I was not one of the ones who loved it. In fact, it's one of the lowest rated books I've ever reviewed here on the blog.<br /><br />Honestly, there is only one thing I liked about Beautiful Disaster and it was the writing. It wasn't spectacularly well written or anything like that but the writing really sped up the book. This is one the few books I've really disliked that I've been able to read in less than a day. Normally if I don't like a book I just stop reading and it takes me forever to get through it. It was the exact opposite with Beautiful Disaster. I could not put the book down. It's kind of like with a train wreck: you know it's going to be bad but you just can't look away. That is basically exactly how I feel about Beautiful Disaster.<br /><br />Let's start with Travis, the super sexy bad boy that everyone seems to love. The first word that comes to mind when I think of Travis is scary. Literally if I met him I would be scared of him. He's mean, he's a bit of a drunk, he's hot-headed and short-tempered, he's brutish, and above all he's just a jerk. There is not a single thing about Travis that I liked. He acted like he owned Abby even when they were supposedly just friends. He beat the crap out of people when they said one wrong word to Abby. He tormented her when she finally found a guy she might want to date. And on top of that he was a clingy, needy guy who couldn't live without Abby. He CRIED at one point when she left him because he was being a stupid jerk. I am perfectly okay with guys crying but this was just taking it to the extreme. He was 100% bad news!<br /><br />As for Abby, she wasn't a whole lot better. In fact, I may have disliked her more than I disliked Travis because she was the one stupid enough to let him get away with all the crap he did to her. She starts off the book saying she'd never fall for a guy like Travis and tries her hardest to stay away from him and make him not want her. Oh and her way of making him not want her is to dress in ratty sweats and a t-shirt with no makeup and flip flops. Umm hello, that's how I dress a lot of the time and I like to think that a guy could still find me attractive dressed like that. That whole thing just didn't make sense to me. You can only be attractive to a guy when you're dressed pretty and covered in makeup? Then there's the whole thing with the bet and her having to live with Travis and sleep in his bed. Okay not so bad, right? Wrong! As soon as she moves in she lets Travis dictate what she does, what she wears, who she hangs out with. She lets him run her life. And as mentioned above when he beats the crap out of people for talking bad about her? She encourages him! What the heck? Why would you encourage your boyfriend to beat someone bloody in the middle of the cafeteria? I'm pretty sure there's a line between defending someone's honor and being a psychopath and Travis crossed that line. Really I could go on and on about the stupid things Abby let Travis do and the stupid things that she did herself but I'm going to stop before this review turns more into a book.<br /><br />As for their whole relationship, I just didn't get it. Travis sleeps with girl after girl and then just decides Abby is the one that he should settle down with? There is no explanation anywhere in the book about what makes her so special. Not once does Travis tell her why exactly he loves her and not all the other billions of girls that he's slept with. At one point Abby has a chance for a healthy relationship with a great guy and she pretty much treats him like shit. She blows him off to hang out with Travis and then at one point she calls him from Travis' phone right after he drops her off from a date to tell him that she's in love with Travis. After spending tons of time denying that to him. Is that really the best way to break it to him? It was a pretty nasty move. Everything about their relationship is just really unhealthy for them both and they know it but they stay together anyway. They are not good for each other and it's clear from the very beginning.<br /><br />Oh and my biggest peeve with this book (which may seem minor to you guys but I couldn't stand it) was the fact that Travis nicknamed Abby \\"Pidgeon\\" or \\"Pidge\\" for short and then gave no explanation why! Seriously, what kind of nickname is that? If my boyfriend ever called me that I would probably laugh in his face and tell him to shut the heck up. It was seriously like Travis didn't even know what her real name was because all he ever called her was Pidgeon or Pidge. It got old really fast and I kind of wanted to just stop paying attention to the story and instead just count how many times he called her that.<br /><br />Overall, I really did not like Beautiful Disaster. I don't recommend it for anyone and I highly suggest you don't waste your money on this one.erything about their relationship is just really unhealthy for them both and they know it but they stay together anyway.
